<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">4 × 100 metres relay</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bolt_se_aposenta_com_medalha_de_ouro_no_4_x_100_metros_1039075-19.08.2016_frz-0955.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2336" data-file-width="3500" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bolt_se_aposenta_com_medalha_de_ouro_no_4_x_100_metros_1039075-19.08.2016_frz-0955.jpg/300px-Bolt_se_aposenta_com_medalha_de_ouro_no_4_x_100_metros_1039075-19.08.2016_frz-0955.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bolt_se_aposenta_com_medalha_de_ouro_no_4_x_100_metros_1039075-19.08.2016_frz-0955.jpg/450px-Bolt_se_aposenta_com_medalha_de_ouro_no_4_x_100_metros_1039075-19.08.2016_frz-0955.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bolt_se_aposenta_com_medalha_de_ouro_no_4_x_100_metros_1039075-19.08.2016_frz-0955.jpg/600px-Bolt_se_aposenta_com_medalha_de_ouro_no_4_x_100_metros_1039075-19.08.2016_frz-0955.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bolt_se_aposenta_com_medalha_de_ouro_no_4_x_100_metros_1039075-19.08.2016_frz-0955.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The finish at the <a href="/wiki/2016_Summer_Olympics" title="2016 Summer Olympics">Rio 2016 Olympics.</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>4 × 100 metres relay</b> or <a href="/wiki/Sprint_(running)" title="Sprint (running)">sprint</a> <a href="/wiki/Relay_race" title="Relay race">relay</a> is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athletics_(sport)" title="Athletics (sport)">athletics</a> <a href="/wiki/Track_and_field" title="Track and field">track</a> event run in lanes over one lap of the track with four runners completing 100 metres each. The first runners must begin in the same stagger as for the individual <a href="/wiki/400_metres" title="400 metres">400 m</a> race. A relay baton is carried by each runner and must be passed within a 20 m changeover box (usually marked by yellow lines) which extends 10 m on either side of each 100 m mark of the race. Another line is marked 10 m further back, marking the earliest point at which the outgoing runner may begin (giving up to 10 m of acceleration before entering the passing zone).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Transferring of the baton in this race is typically blind. The outgoing runner reaches a straight arm backwards when they enter the changeover box, or when the incoming runner makes a verbal signal. The outgoing runner does not look backwards, and it is the responsibility<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> of the incoming runner to thrust the baton into the outstretched hand, and not let go until the outgoing runner takes hold of it. Runners on the first and third legs typically run on the inside of the lane with the baton in their right hand, while runners on the second and fourth legs take the baton in their left. Polished handovers can compensate for a lack of basic speed to some extent, and disqualification for dropping the baton or failing to transfer it within the box is common, even at the highest level.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> men historically dominated this event through the 20th Century, having won 15 Olympic gold medals and 5 IAAF world championships. <a href="/wiki/Carl_Lewis" title="Carl Lewis">Carl Lewis</a> ran the <a href="/wiki/Anchor_leg" title="Anchor leg">anchor leg</a> on five U.S relay teams that broke the World Record from 1983–92, the first team to break 38 seconds.</p>
<p>The current men's <a href="/wiki/List_of_world_records_in_athletics" title="List of world records in athletics">world record</a> stands at 36.84 as set by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jamaican" title="Jamaican">Jamaican</a> team at the <a href="/wiki/Athletics_at_the_2012_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Men%27s_4_%C3%97_100_metres_relay" title="Athletics at the 2012 Summer Olympics – Men's 4 × 100 metres relay">2012 London Olympic games</a> on 11 August 2012. As the only team to break 37 seconds to date, Jamaica has been the dominant team in the sport, winning 3 consecutive Olympic Gold Medals as well as 4 consecutive World Championships. The previous record was 37.04 seconds as set by the <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaican</a> team at the <a href="/wiki/2011_World_Championships_in_Athletics_%E2%80%93_Men%27s_4_%C3%97_100_metres_relay" title="2011 World Championships in Athletics – Men's 4 × 100 metres relay">2011 World Championships</a>.</p>
<p>The fastest electronically timed anchor leg run is 8.65 seconds by <a href="/wiki/Usain_Bolt" title="Usain Bolt">Usain Bolt</a> at the <a href="/wiki/2015_IAAF_World_Relays_%E2%80%93_Men%27s_4_%C3%97_100_metres_relay" title="2015 IAAF World Relays – Men's 4 × 100 metres relay">2015 IAAF World Relays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bob_Hayes" title="Bob Hayes">Bob Hayes</a> was hand-timed as running between 8.5 and 8.9 seconds on a cinder track at the <a href="/wiki/Athletics_at_the_1964_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Men%27s_4_%C3%97_100_metres_relay" title="Athletics at the 1964 Summer Olympics – Men's 4 × 100 metres relay">1964 Tokyo Games</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the women's event, the United States was considered by many<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2013)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> to be a strong team after years of <a href="/wiki/East_Germany#Sport" title="East Germany">East German</a> domination, but failed to make it to the finals in the 2008 Olympic Summer Games. <a href="/wiki/Carmelita_Jeter" title="Carmelita Jeter">Carmelita Jeter</a> ran an anchor leg of 9.70 seconds, the fastest ever in London Olympics 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The women's world record stands at 40.82 seconds, set by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_of_America" title="United States of America">United States of America</a> in 2012 at the <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics" title="2012 Summer Olympics">London Olympics</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<ul>
<li><sup>dq2</sup> The United States team of <a href="/wiki/Kelli_White" title="Kelli White">Kelli White</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chryste_Gaines" title="Chryste Gaines">Chryste Gaines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inger_Miller" title="Inger Miller">Inger Miller</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marion_Jones" title="Marion Jones">Marion Jones</a> originally won the 2001 World Championship in a time of 41.71 seconds, but were disqualified after Jones admitted to drug use as a result of the BALCO scandal in 2005.</li>
</ul>
